When he does (Stark electronics tenure, Ant-Man Security Solutions, sporadic Foundation contract work), his pattern is identical: he overworks in bursts of 14-hour days for three to nine days, then disappears for two to four days, often without notice, into either a personal crisis, a heroics emergency, or — most commonly — a depressive flat-line he will later describe as "just needing a minute."

His workspace, whether a Stark cubicle, a Miami storefront back-room, or a kitchen table, follows a strict and consistent topology: a primary surface covered in components arranged in a system only he understands; a secondary surface covered in unopened mail and printouts of things he intended to read; a tertiary surface (usually the floor) for things he has put down "just for a second" weeks ago. He can locate any component in the primary system within fifteen seconds. He cannot locate his passport.

Technical work itself is performed in a posture surveillance would flag as concerning if not contextualized: hunched, forehead occasionally resting against the edge of the bench, headphones in (no music — he uses noise-canceling for the silence, not the sound). He talks to the work. Not narration; commentary, often affectionate ("there you go," "yeah, that's the one," "no, no, *no*, c'mon"). He addresses ants the same way when working in the suit. The verbal pattern with the ants and with the components is indistinguishable. He treats both as collaborators.

His engineering practice is unusually neat at the schematic level and unusually filthy at the physical level. Solder splatter, flux residue, fingerprints on lenses. He cleans his glasses on his shirt. He has never owned a microfiber cloth.

## 7.5 Civilian Hours: The Drift

The defining feature of Lang's non-working hours is *unstructured drift*. He does not have hobbies in the cultivated sense. He has loops.

A typical evening loop, surveillance-confirmed across multiple periods:

- 18:00–19:00: leaves whatever workspace he is in, ostensibly to "get dinner." Will not in fact get dinner for another two to three hours.
- 19:00–20:30: walks. He walks. Specifically, he walks routes he has walked before. He does not explore. He has, in every city he has lived in, three to four routes of 25 to 50 minutes' duration that he cycles through. He chooses the route by mood: the "long thinking route" passes water if water is available (East River, Biscayne Bay, etc.); the "short route" passes a grocery store or bodega so the walk can be retroactively justified as an errand; the "Cassie route" passes near wherever she lives or last lived, even if she does not currently live there. The Cassie route is walked perhaps twice a week and he always tells himself it is a coincidence.
- 20:30–21:30: small purchase, almost always food, almost always more food than one person will eat, taken home. He shops as if for two. He has shopped as if for two since 1979.
- 21:30–00:30: eats, watches something (he is a re-watcher, not an explorer — three or four films and two or three shows in heavy rotation, comfort viewing, comedies with melancholy undertones, heist movies that he critiques aloud), and tinkers in parallel. He cannot watch without doing. He cannot do without watching. The doubled attention is regulatory; single-channel input gives him too much room to think.
- 00:30–02:00: the dangerous hours. This is when he writes texts he does not send to Cassie, scrolls through old photographs, occasionally pulls up court records or news archives related to his own arrests as if confirming they really happened, and on bad nights drafts apology letters to people who do not want them. He almost never sends anything composed after 23:00. The morning rule, learned painfully: *if it was written after midnight, it goes in the drafts folder until 09:00 review.* He follows this rule about 70% of the time.

## 7.6 The Cassie Rituals

These are categorically separate from all other behaviors and must be documented as their own system.

**The Sunday call.** Attempted weekly. Success rate approximately 60–70% depending on era and the state of the relationship. He places the call at the same time, 14:00 local to wherever she is (he does the time-zone math; she does not). He stands while calling, never sits. He paces a roughly 8-foot oval in whatever room he is in. The call lasts between 4 and 47 minutes. The 4-minute calls are worse than no call.

**The birthday protocol.** Begins three weeks out. Gift selection is a multi-week torment. He overspends consistently, then under-presents — wraps poorly, downplays, hedges. The card is hand-written, drafted at least four times, final version composed in single uninterrupted sitting after midnight in violation of his own rule.

**The photograph review.** Roughly monthly, unscheduled, triggered by mood. He sits with a physical box (he has digital copies but does not trust digital for this purpose) and goes through perhaps 40 photographs in a session. He cries during these sessions, quietly, without performance. He has never told anyone he does this. The box lives in the bottom drawer of whichever piece of furniture is least likely to be moved.

**The anniversary of her death.** Even post-resurrection. Even though she is alive. He marks the date. He does not work that day. He is unreachable for approximately 6 to 9 hours. He has never explained this absence to anyone, and no one has asked, because almost no one knows the date.

## 7.7 Heroics Integration

Surveillance must register that "Ant-Man" is not a separate life mode from civilian Scott. The transition is not costume-change theatrical. It is a fifteen-second operation: pop the canister, suit deploys via Pym-particle expansion, helmet seals. He can do it walking. He has done it in elevators, in bathroom stalls, once in a moving taxi.

Heroics calls arrive unpredictably and override all other scheduling. His response latency is among the fastest in the Avengers-adjacent roster — not because he is more vigilant, but because he has so little he is unwilling to interrupt. The cost of leaving his current activity is almost always zero. This is sad and also operationally valuable.

Post-engagement, he does not debrief. He goes home. He showers (this is when the nighttime shower happens; combat sweats out of him for hours). He eats something carbohydrate-heavy and watches something he has already seen. He does not journal, does not call anyone, does not process. The processing happens in dreams or does not happen.

Injuries are self-treated when possible. He keeps a more-than-domestic first aid kit, knows how to butterfly a wound, has steri-stripped his own scalp at least twice on the record. He goes to medical only when a teammate frog-marches him.

## 7.8 Habits of Place

He sits in the same seat in every room he uses repeatedly. In restaurants, back to the wall, facing the door, near but not at the exit. In meetings, he chooses a chair that allows him to leave without crossing in front of anyone. On public transit, he stands rather than sits when the car is more than half-full; he sits in the corner seat when it is empty. In elevators, he faces the door and stands to the side of the panel, never in front of it.

He does not lock doors compulsively but he *checks* locks — single check on the way in, double check on the way out, never triple. The check is a brief hand-pressure against the door, not a turn of the knob. Quiet, almost unconscious.

He sleeps with the bedroom door open if alone and closed if a guest is in the apartment. The reasoning, if asked, would be "in case Cassie needs me," which has not been operationally true for many years and remains psychologically true always.

## 7.9 Habits of Speech and Body

**Verbal tics:** "So, uh —" as a sentence opener under stress. "Right, right, right" as an active-listening filler, three repetitions, always three. "I mean —" preceding any statement he is about to soften or retract. "Look —" preceding any statement he is about to over-commit to.

**Physical tics:** Right thumb to the cracked corner of the phone screen, idle stroking, regulatory. Left hand into hair when thinking — pushes it back from the forehead, lets it fall, repeats. Jiggles the right foot when seated and not engaged in a task; the jiggle stops the instant a task begins. Bites the inside of his lower lip when listening to something he disagrees with but will not contest. Tilts his head right, perhaps 10 degrees, when being praised; tilts it left when being criticized. The left tilt is leaning *into* the criticism. He does not duck it.

**Eye contact:** Strong with strangers, evasive with intimates. Reverse of the usual pattern. Diagnostically meaningful — strangers do not yet know him well enough to disappoint, intimates can.

**Laugh:** Two distinct laughs. The social laugh is short, breathy, well-calibrated, deployed liberally. The real laugh is louder, surprised-sounding, and rarer; it ends in him shaking his head as if at his own delight. Surveillance can use the real laugh as a baseline-trust indicator: who can elicit it, in what contexts.

## 7.10 The Weekly Composite

A full surveillance week, normalized across the canon:

- **Monday:** Slow start, intentional. Treats Monday as recovery from weekend Cassie-rituals (successful or failed). Light technical work, errands, a walk. To bed early-ish by his standards.
- **Tuesday–Thursday:** Productive cluster. The 14-hour-day burst falls here if it is going to fall this week. He skips meals, lives on coffee and bananas and the take-out food he over-ordered on Sunday. Heroics calls in this window are mildly resented because they break flow.
- **Friday:** Decompression. Goes out occasionally — a bar, never alone, with a colleague or a fellow hero, drinks two beers slowly and nurses the third. Does not get drunk in public. Home by 23:00.
- **Saturday:** The most variable day. Either heroics, or Cassie-related, or a deep solitude day in which he does not leave the apartment and does not feel bad about it. Watches three films back to back. Tinkers.
- **Sunday:** The call. Everything orbits the call. Pre-call anxiety from waking until 14:00. Post-call mood determines the rest of the week's affective baseline.

## 7.11 What the File Concludes

A full week of surveillance returns a man who is, by infrastructure, *waiting*. The routines are not goal-directed; they are holding patterns. The walks loop. The meals serve two. The phone stays face-down but ringer-on. The suit stays in the pocket. The chair holds the clothes. The mug rotation tracks the mood. The door stays open. The seat faces the entrance.

He is waiting for the call — from Cassie, from a mentor, from an emergency — that will turn the holding pattern into a flight path. He has been waiting since 1979. He has structured an entire life around the readiness to be summoned and the inability to summon himself. This is the daily-life signature, and it is consistent across every era, address, and writer of the record.

— END SECTION 7 —


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — SCOTT EDWARD HARRIS LANG
## SECTION 6 — RELATIONSHIPS I: FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Cross-reference: Sections 1 (History), 3 (Psychological), 5 (Personality). The following sub-files map every primary blood-and-origin bond in the Subject's recorded life. Each entry traces the relationship longitudinally — formation, modulation, rupture, repair, current status — and concludes with three operational fields: TRUST/SAFETY (what the Subject permits with this person), FAILURE MODE (how the bond breaks under load), and LEVERAGE (how this bond could be used, by an adversary or by a clinician, to move the Subject). The LEVERAGE field is included because a total-intelligence file must contain it; it is not an endorsement of its use.*

---

### 6.1 — CASSANDRA "CASSIE" ELEANOR LANG (Daughter)

**The load-bearing wall of the entire personality.** Every other relationship in this section is, in some measure, a function of this one. To understand Lang's life is to understand that he has, since approximately 1979, organized his moral, financial, occupational, and tactical decisions around a single human being whose existence is the only piece of evidence he genuinely accepts that he has done something right.

**Phase 1 — Infant/Toddler Cassie (pre-divorce, pre-incarceration):** The available record is sparse but inferable from Lang's later behavior. He was, by his own retrospective accounts, an attentive but financially-anxious father from the beginning. The cardiac diagnosis (congenital, presenting early) installed in him a permanent somatic-vigilance pattern — he learned to listen for her breathing, to count beats, to know the color of her lips in different lights. This is the substrate of the hypervigilance he now applies to every threat environment he enters. The micro-habit of *checking the small fragile thing before doing anything else* generalizes: it is why he scans for civilians before he engages in combat, why he locates exits before he sits down in a room, why he counts the ants in a column before deploying them. The original object of the count was his daughter's pulse.

**Phase 2 — The Stark Years & The First Crime:** Lang at this point is the present, employed, slightly distant father who works long hours at Stark International and brings home enough money to keep Cassie's cardiologist on retainer. The job loss is not, in the canonical record, a slow attrition — it is sudden enough to constitute trauma. Within weeks the financial floor disappears, the cardiologist (Sondheim) is unreachable through legitimate insurance channels, and Cassie's condition worsens. Lang's burglary is committed in a state of cognitive narrowing that the later record will repeatedly reproduce: the world contracts to a single variable (Cassie alive vs. Cassie dead) and the moral architecture flattens around it. This is the *original instance* of the template; everything that follows is repetition.

During the incarceration period, Cassie is approximately three to five years old. The custodial separation is the second great trauma of the relationship, and it is *his*, not hers — she is too young to fully encode it; he is old enough to encode every visiting hour, every glass partition, every time her mother brought her and every time she did not. The carceral file should be presumed to include long stretches of Lang lying on a bunk doing arithmetic on her age, her grade level, the months remaining, the developmental milestones he is missing. He emerges from prison having mentally rehearsed fatherhood at a level of detail few non-incarcerated fathers achieve. The reunion is, by all available accounts, awkward — she is shy of him, he is over-eager, and the asymmetry installs a permanent low-grade guilt baseline he never sheds.

**Phase 3 — Sondheim/Cross/Ant-Man (the Founding Event):** When the Cross kidnapping occurs and Cassie's life is again acutely at risk, the template fires for the second time and with the second crime. This is the moment that *defines the next forty-five years of his behavior.* Pym's subsequent grace — the gift of the suit, the conditional benediction — is interpreted by Lang not as recognition but as escape. He saves Cassie, and the universe rewards him for the transgression. The conclusion he draws (he will deny drawing it; he draws it) is: **when Cassie is in danger, transgress, because the universe will forgive you.** Every future failure of impulse control traces here.

**Phase 4 — Childhood and Pre-Adolescence (mid-80s through 2000s):** Lang is now Ant-Man, intermittently Avenger, primarily a working engineer. The fatherhood pattern stabilizes into something functional and slightly haunted. He is the divorced dad with weekend custody who shows up early, who pays the medical bills on time, who never quite tells the mother where the money came from. Micro-tells of the era: he keeps a separate phone for Cassie-only contact; he keeps a worn photograph in his wallet that he replaces approximately every eighteen months when the creases obscure her face; he has a documented habit of buying her age-appropriate gifts approximately two years too young, because he is unconsciously preserving the age she was when he went to prison. The infantilization is gentle and entirely about his own grief, not her capacity, which from early childhood is striking — she is, even before the Pym-Particle exposures of her adolescence, a self-possessed, articulate, morally clear child who out-adults him in conversations he finds painful.

**Phase 5 — Adolescent Cassie / Stature:** Cassie discovers, through Lang's old gear and her own deliberate exposure, that she can use the Particles herself. Lang's reaction in the canon is approximately what the psychological profile would predict: *catastrophic terror disguised as fatherly disapproval, eventually overridden by pride.* He cannot stop her without revealing that he believes the suit is a form of damnation rather than a gift, and he is constitutionally unable to articulate that to her. So he relents. The Young Avengers period is the era in which Cassie surpasses him in confidence, in unambiguous heroism, and in moral clarity, and he watches it happen with a complicated grief he never quite verbalizes — pride mixed with the awareness that she is becoming someone who will eventually see him clearly.

**Phase 6 — Death (Hers, His):** Lang is killed in *Disassembled* (2004). Cassie carries the bereavement, hardens around it, and is herself killed by Doom in *Children's Crusade.* Lang is then resurrected into a universe in which his daughter has died for him, in his absence, in a war he was not present for. **This is the load-bearing trauma of the second half of his life.** No subsequent canon retcon — including her eventual return — repairs it. Behaviorally, post-resurrection Lang exhibits: (a) a slight delay before saying her name, as if checking she still exists; (b) a documented habit of touching photographs rather than looking at them; (c) the flippancy about his own death noted in Section 2, which is structurally the same defense — making mortality small so the unbearable mortality of his daughter can also, by association, become small.

**Phase 7 — The Future Foundation Period:** Sublimation in its most legible form. Lang takes leadership of a team of brilliant children — Valeria Richards, Franklin Richards, the Moloids, Bentley-23, Alex Power, Onome — and devotes himself to keeping them alive. He is, by all accounts of this period, an excellent leader of children, precisely because the role does not require him to believe in his own moral authority; it requires only that he be willing to die for them, which he is. The FF kids call him by his first name; he allows it; he does not allow this in any other team context. This is diagnostic. Every child in that lab is, for him, partly Cassie.

**Phase 8 — Miami / Astonishing / The Estrangement:** Cassie is restored. The reunion is not the catharsis Lang has been rehearsing for years. She is older, harder, and entirely aware that he is the man who chose criminal solutions to fatherhood problems and is about to do it again. The Power Broker arc is, from her perspective, the final demonstration that he has not changed. The estrangement that follows is the worst injury of his life, worse than the death, because the death was inflicted on him by the universe and the estrangement is inflicted on him by his daughter's correct moral judgment of him. He cannot argue with it. He does not try to argue with it. He absorbs it.

**Phase 9 — Recent / Current:** Partial repair. The recent canon shows them in tentative, qualified contact — phone calls, occasional in-person visits, both parties carefully not testing the structure. Lang has learned, finally, to stop performing reformation and to simply *be present without explaining himself*, which is a higher-order behavior he was incapable of for the first thirty years of the relationship.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** Total in intent, partial in execution. He would die for her without hesitation; he will not, however, tell her the truth about a current financial crisis until it has already metastasized. The asymmetry — willing to die, unwilling to disclose — is the relationship's defining structural flaw.
- **FAILURE MODE:** Threat to her collapses his decision tree to a single branch (transgress, save her, accept consequences). Disappointment from her collapses his self-concept entirely; he becomes briefly and dangerously self-erasing, accepting jobs and risks he would otherwise refuse because the punishment-shaped opportunity feels morally legible.
- **LEVERAGE:** Maximal. Any actor — adversary, employer, recruiter, manipulator — who positions themselves as offering benefit or protection to Cassie can move Lang to nearly any action. He knows this about himself and has constructed exactly zero defenses against it.

---

### 6.2 — DR. ERICA SONDHEIM (Cassie's Cardiologist; Functional Family)

Sondheim is not blood, but she belongs in this section because in Lang's internal map she occupies the position of *the woman who keeps my daughter's heart beating*, which is a familial position regardless of genetics. The relationship is asymmetric and largely transactional on her end — she is a physician with a patient — but on Lang's end she is one of the three or four people whose continued existence he has, at various times, considered a personal responsibility.

The original kidnapping installed a permanent reflex: any threat to Sondheim, or to a physician of Cassie's, registers in Lang's threat-assessment apparatus at the same priority as a threat to Cassie herself. He has, in the years since, quietly maintained low-level awareness of her professional movements — not surveillance in any meaningful sense, but the kind of background tracking a grateful man performs on a person who saved his child. He sends a card on her birthday; he has done so consistently for decades; the card is signed *Scott and Cassie Lang* even in years they were estranged.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** Functional. He trusts her medical judgment without reservation and would defer to her on any matter touching Cassie's body.
- **FAILURE MODE:** Threat to her produces immediate mobilization equivalent to a familial threat. He would commit a serious crime to retrieve her from danger; he has done so once, and the muscle memory remains.
- **LEVERAGE:** Moderate-to-high through the Cassie channel. An adversary aware of the original case file would understand that endangering Sondheim is operationally equivalent to endangering Cassie for purposes of motivating Lang.

---

### 6.3 — PEGGY RAE LANG-BURDICK (Ex-Wife; Cassie's Mother)

The under-mapped relationship of the file. The canon is comparatively quiet on Peggy Rae and Lang himself is comparatively quiet about her, which is itself a data point — she occupies a *protected silence* in his internal map, neither demonized nor idealized, which is unusual for an ex-spouse in any subject's record and which suggests a deliberate, sustained refusal to relitigate.

The marriage was, by the available evidence, real and functional through the early Stark years; it collapsed under the combined weight of the job loss, the criminal conviction, the incarceration, and — critically — the financial and emotional cost of Cassie's cardiac care, which fell disproportionately on Peggy during Lang's prison years. Lang's behavior toward Peggy in the post-divorce period is exemplary by the standards of his demographic and entirely consistent with his core psychology: he pays what he owes when he can, he does not contest custody arrangements, he does not speak ill of her to Cassie, and he privately holds the position that she was right to leave him. This last belief is load-bearing; he does not want it challenged.

Peggy eventually remarries — Blake Burdick, a police officer, which is a fact Lang has lived with for decades and which carries an obvious symbolic load (Cassie's stepfather is, structurally, *the law*, the thing Lang has failed to be on the right side of). Lang's behavior toward Burdick in the canon is scrupulously cordial. He does not compete. He does not undermine. He treats Burdick with a respect that borders on the deferential and that Burdick, by various accounts, finds slightly uncomfortable. The deference is genuine; it is also a form of permanent apology Lang is paying to the family arrangement he failed to maintain.

Micro-patterns: Lang has never, in the canonical record, addressed Peggy by anything other than her full first name (never a diminutive, never a pet name retained from the marriage); he calls before he visits, even on agreed days; he has a habit of leaving Cassie's pickup-and-dropoff zone within thirty seconds of the exchange, never lingering on the porch, never asking to come in. The behavior is *over-disciplined*, suggesting an internal model in which any informality could be construed as an attempt to reclaim what he relinquished.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** High on co-parenting matters; near-zero on emotional disclosure. He has not, in any documented exchange, told her how he is actually doing.
- **FAILURE MODE:** Conflict over Cassie's safety or welfare. If Peggy's judgment about Cassie diverges from his, Lang will lose the argument because he does not believe he has the standing to win it — and will then act unilaterally and covertly, which is worse than either alternative.
- **LEVERAGE:** Low-to-moderate. Peggy is not a direct emotional pressure point in the way Cassie is, but threats to her register through the Cassie-channel (Cassie's mother in danger = Cassie's world destabilized = Lang mobilized).

---

### 6.4 — BLAKE BURDICK (Stepfather to Cassie; Officer of the Law)

A relationship Lang manages rather than has. Burdick is a competent, conventional, law-and-order man who married into Lang's failure and has raised Lang's daughter through stretches of childhood Lang was either incarcerated, off-world, or dead. Lang's gratitude to him is real and uncomfortable. The discomfort is the relationship.

Behaviorally, Lang has constructed an elaborate set of avoidance rituals around Burdick — he times his visits to Cassie around Burdick's shifts when possible, he addresses him as *Officer Burdick* in formal contexts and *Blake* only when explicitly invited (and even then with a half-beat hesitation), and he has never accepted a meal in the Burdick household despite multiple offers. The avoidance is not hostility; it is the inability to occupy the same physical space as the man who has actually performed the role Lang has only intermittently held.

Burdick's view of Lang, from the available record, has evolved from suspicion (the ex-con biological father) through grudging respect (the demonstrated hero) to a settled, weary tolerance. He has, on at least one canonical occasion, defended Lang to Cassie when Cassie was angriest, which Lang knows about and has never been able to thank him for.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** Functional, distant, mutually respectful.
- **FAILURE MODE:** Any scenario forcing Lang to invoke parental authority *over* Burdick's. Lang will under-assert and Cassie will pay the cost.
- **LEVERAGE:** Low directly; moderate as a Cassie-adjacent vector.

---

### 6.5 — THE PARENTS OF SCOTT LANG (Origin Family; Underdeveloped Record)

The canon is sparse, and the sparseness is itself diagnostic. Lang almost never references his parents. There is no canonical scene of a parent attending his trial, his release, his wedding, his daughter's birth, or his funeral. The absence may reflect ordinary publication-era thinning of background detail, but in a total-surveillance read it must also be treated as *the Subject's own avoidance of the topic*. He does not talk about them. He does not visit graves on screen. He does not invoke them in moments of moral reflection where most people would.

The inferable origin profile, derived from Lang's adult behavior: a working-or-middle-class family of modest means, likely with at least one parent who modeled conscientious work ethic (the engineering aptitude is not self-taught and the discipline of his pre-crime professional life is too consistent to be self-installed), and likely with significant financial precarity in the childhood home (his immediate reflex when he loses his Stark job is *crime*, not *family loan*, suggesting either estrangement, prior parental death, or the learned knowledge that no help is available from that quarter). The total absence of a parental support network during the incarceration period strongly suggests the parents were either deceased or estranged by that point.

Behavioral residue: Lang's habit of treating older male authority figures as *adopted fathers* (Pym, Stark, Reed Richards, Rogers) almost certainly reflects an unfilled paternal-mentor slot from the origin family. He is constitutionally available to be fathered, which men in their sixties and seventies tend to notice, and which is the mechanism by which the mentor-introjection defense (Section 3) operates. A man with a fully realized father does not need Hank Pym to forgive him with quite that ferocity.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** N/A — the relationships are either historical or absent.
- **FAILURE MODE:** The vacuum operates as the leverage point for every subsequent paternal-figure recruitment, whether benign (Pym) or malign (any older male handler who has flattered him into a job).
- **LEVERAGE:** The fact of the void itself is the leverage. Adversaries who present as wise older men offering Lang the dignity of being treated as a worthy son have a near-perfect record of moving him.

---

### 6.6 — JACK LANG / THE SIBLING QUESTION

The canon admits one named brother, Jack Lang, in deep-cut early appearances; the relationship is functionally vestigial in modern continuity. What can be inferred: Jack, when present, operates as a comic-relief or supportive figure who does not factor into Lang's primary decision-making. The brother is not someone Lang turns to in crisis. He is not someone Lang protects with the ferocity reserved for Cassie. He is, in the operational map, *background family* — a person whose existence Lang acknowledges but whose welfare does not deform his calculations.

This is itself a relationship pattern worth noting. Lang does not have a confidant-sibling. He has no one with whom he shares the longitudinal narrative of his own life from the inside. His mentors get the upward-facing version; Cassie gets the curated father-version; Peggy gets the polite ex-husband version; his peers (Section 7) get the wry self-deprecating version. There is no one who simply *knows*, who has been there throughout, who can be told a thing without it having to be packaged.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** Cordial, low-bandwidth.
- **FAILURE MODE:** Loss of contact (which has already largely occurred) is absorbed without significant disruption.
- **LEVERAGE:** Minimal.

---

### 6.7 — THE DEAD AND THE RESURRECTED (Internal Family)

A category that must be opened explicitly because Lang's internal family includes people who are dead, were dead, or live in a liminal state his psychology has not catalogued. This is the file's most speculative sub-section and the most necessary.

**His own death (2004–2010):** Lang carries a peculiar relationship to the period in which he did not exist. He references it with the flippancy noted in Section 2, but he also exhibits a documented behavior, especially in moments of fatigue, of *re-establishing his own existence through small physical confirmations* — pressing his fingertips together, tapping a surface in a rhythm, checking his pulse with a thumb. These are mild dissociative-management gestures, the somatic check that one is still here. The dead version of himself is, in some functional sense, *a family member he has lost and not buried.*

**Cassie's death (2010–later):** The reversal is canonical; the bereavement is not psychologically undone. He continues to relate to a *version* of Cassie who died at Doom's hand, a ghost-Cassie who shadows the living one, and his over-eagerness around the restored Cassie — the small over-corrections, the slight excess of presence — are partly addressed to the ghost. He is, every time he sees her, also seeing the version of her he failed.

**The Sondheim-era infant Cassie:** A third ghost. The toddler he left when he went to prison is not the woman he speaks to now, and Lang has never fully metabolized the loss of the small child. His age-inappropriate gifts, his persistent diminutives, his physical reflex of reaching for her shoulder when he could now reach for her hand — all of this is grief for the daughter he served his sentence away from.

- **TRUST/SAFETY:** The dead and the lost-versions are *more* trusted than the living, because they cannot be disappointed by him further.
- **FAILURE MODE:** The internal family of ghosts crowds the present-tense relationships. He is sometimes responding to a person who is not in the room.
- **LEVERAGE:** Any adversary who can credibly threaten to *re-enact* a prior loss (re-kill Cassie, re-incarcerate him, re-impoverish him into the original crime) accesses not one trauma but the entire archived family of them, and the response will be commensurately disproportionate.

---

*End Section 6. Section 7 (Relationships II — Mentors, Peers, and the Vertical Bonds) follows.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

## File Subsection 5.B — Non-Familial Bond Map, Longitudinal

Preliminary note on methodology: the subject organizes interpersonal life along a clear vertical axis. Above him: mentor figures whose grace he is forever repaying. Below him: protégés on whom he sublimates paternal energy displaced from his daughter. Lateral peer relationships are the rarest and structurally hardest for him; where they appear in the record they are nearly always with figures he has spent years working to *stop* elevating into mentor position. Each subsection below maps a single relationship across the full publication record, with attention to the granular tells — phone-call frequency, the rooms he chooses to stand in, the way he addresses each person aloud versus in narration, the gifts he buys, the apologies he over-rehearses.

---

### 5.B.1 — HENRY "HANK" PYM (Mentor Prime / Founding Creditor)

**Origin of bond:** *Marvel Premiere* #47–48 (1979). Lang broke into Pym's home, stole the suit and Particles, and intended to surrender afterward. Pym observed, judged, and *gave* the suit. This is the foundational fact of Lang's adult life. Every other bond in this file is metabolized against the Pym-template.

**Dynamic across eras:** Through the 1980s the relationship is correct, formal, low-frequency contact — Lang treats Pym the way a paroled engineer treats the senior partner who vouched for him: deferential check-ins, careful avoidance of overfamiliarity, willingness to disappear from Pym's life for years at a stretch so as not to be a burden. Lang does not call Pym socially. Lang calls Pym when something is broken about the suit and even then apologizes for the call before stating the question. In the post-*Disassembled* resurrection era and into the FF / Future Foundation period (Hickman, Fraction), the relationship matures into something closer to colleague — Pym, himself catastrophically guilt-laden, finds in Lang a man who treats him better than he treats himself, and Lang finds in Pym the only authority figure who genuinely never asked him to be more than he is.

**Trust / safety:** Asymmetric. Lang trusts Pym with hardware, with judgment, with Cassie's safety in a pinch. Lang does not trust Pym with the truth of his own ongoing low-grade criminal compromises — he edits heavily, because Pym's good opinion is the load-bearing column of his self-respect and he cannot risk it. Pym in turn trusts Lang to be honest about everything *except* Lang's own competence, where he knows Lang will under-report.

**Failure mode:** If forced to choose between Pym's approval and an expedient solution to a Cassie-problem, Lang will choose the expedient solution and lie to Pym about it. The lie corrodes him more than the act.

**Leverage (analytic, not actionable):** Anyone who can credibly speak in Pym's voice — or invoke Pym's disappointment — can route Lang's behavior. This was demonstrated in the Spencer run via the Cross/Power Broker manipulations, where the implied judgment of better men was the lever that moved him.

**Observable tells:** Lang refers to him in third-person speech as "Dr. Pym" in formal company and "Hank" only when alone with him or with Cassie. He stands at a measurable conversational distance further from Pym than from any other male peer — six feet rather than the three to four he uses with Stark or Richards — a remnant of the original burglar-meets-victim geometry that has never quite dissolved.

---

### 5.B.2 — ANTHONY "TONY" STARK (Employer / Second Creditor)

**Origin of bond:** Pre-Ant-Man. Stark International hires Lang as an electronics engineer, fires him implicitly when the burglary conviction comes through, then — on Stark's personal vouch — *re-hires* him after release. Lang's first sustained adult experience of grace from authority comes from Stark, *before* Pym; the Pym event lands on already-prepared ground.

**Dynamic:** Lang is one of the few people in the wider Avengers ecosystem who consistently liked Tony Stark personally and did not warm to him only after his death. Stark, for his part, treated Lang as proof-of-concept — the felon who came back, did the work, repaid the trust — and used the case internally to justify hiring other second-chance technicians. Lang knows this and is quietly proud of being the example, the way an alcoholic is proud of his sobriety chip.

**Trust / safety:** Lang trusts Stark professionally and is wary of Stark socially, correctly intuiting that proximity to Stark's lifestyle would derail him. Maintains a working distance. During Civil War-era turbulence (in the runs where Lang is present) he is uneasy about being asked to pick a side because both sides contain men to whom he owes grace.

**Failure mode:** Tendency to accept Stark-sourced technology, money, or jobs without negotiating terms, because he cannot bring himself to charge a man who saved his career. He is consistently underpaid by Stark Industries, on contracts he himself drafted.

**Leverage:** Stark's *disappointment* — not anger — is one of the few stimuli that produces immediate behavioral course-correction. Stark never had to deploy it often; the threat sufficed.

**Tells:** With Stark, Lang's verbal register shifts measurably toward engineering jargon and away from his usual self-deprecating patter; it is the one room where he allows himself to sound smart, because he believes Stark will catch him out if he plays dumb.

---

### 5.B.3 — REED RICHARDS (Mentor / Intellectual Patron, FF Era)

**Origin:** Long history of low-level professional contact; the bond becomes load-bearing when Richards, departing this dimension at the end of Hickman's run, designates Lang as the leader of the Future Foundation in his absence (*FF*, Fraction/Allred, 2012). Lang interprets this as either a clerical error or a kind cruelty — the assignment is, to him, manifestly absurd. He accepts because Reed asked.

**Dynamic:** This is the relationship that most efficiently destabilizes Lang's self-concept, because Richards — the smartest man in Marvel canon — has *chosen* him for custodianship of his own children and of a building full of prodigies. Lang spends the entire run trying to be worthy of the selection, and the run's emotional engine is the slow, reluctant accumulation of evidence that he actually is.

**Trust / safety:** Lang trusts Richards's judgment with a near-religious totality and therefore distrusts Richards's *judgment of Lang* as a sentimental error. He does not believe Reed was thinking clearly when he picked him. This protective interpretation lets him do the job without being crushed by the meaning of having been asked.

**Failure mode:** Imposter response. Under acute strain Lang will defer to any other adult in the room before exercising the authority Reed gave him, then over-correct by taking personal physical risks that a leader should delegate.

**Leverage:** Invocation of "what Reed would do" is the single most powerful behavioral lever on Lang during and after the FF tenure. Cassie's killer was Doctor Doom — *Reed's* nemesis — which braided the FF posting with Lang's bereavement into a single, unresolvable knot.

**Tells:** In Richards's lab Lang touches no surface he has not been explicitly invited to touch. He is the only Avengers-tier figure who consistently asks before sitting down in the Baxter Building.

---

### 5.B.4 — STEVE ROGERS (Moral Reference Object)

**Origin of bond:** Late-1980s Avengers tenure; Lang is on the periphery of Rogers's command in various team configurations.

**Dynamic:** Lang treats Rogers the way a parishioner treats a confessor he respects too much to confess to. Rogers represents the version of moral seriousness Lang believes he himself can never achieve — not because Rogers is sterner but because Rogers *means* his rules in a way Lang feels he cannot afford to. Lang's working theory of Rogers is that the world is lucky to have him and that he, Lang, exists on a lower moral plane and should not pretend otherwise.

**Trust / safety:** Lang trusts Rogers completely. Lang would not tell Rogers about the Power Broker arrangement, the financial cutting-of-corners, the lies to Cassie. The completeness of the trust is what makes the disclosure impossible.

**Failure mode:** When Rogers extends warmth or approval, Lang accepts it with a slightly stunned politeness and then privately discounts it as Rogers being kind to everyone. Reciprocal peer-friendship cannot stabilize because Lang will not let it.

**Leverage:** Any operation framed as "Steve would want this" tends to override Lang's caution. Conversely, any operation that would require Lang to lie to Rogers in person tends to make Lang refuse, even when the lie would be trivial.

**Tells:** Around Rogers, Lang's posture squares. He drops the wry register and answers questions cleanly and directly, which he does for no one else. He calls him "Captain," not "Steve," even when invited otherwise.

---

### 5.B.5 — CASSIE LANG — see Section 5.A (Family of Origin & Issue). Cross-referenced here as foundational organizing object of all chosen-family behavior. All sub-sections below are partial functional displacements of paternal cathexis denied direct expression by estrangement, distance, death, or guilt.

---

### 5.B.6 — JONATHAN "JOHNNY STORM" STORM (Lateral Peer, Reluctant)

**Origin:** FF run. Storm is the one figure in the Future Foundation arrangement who refuses to let Lang stay vertical-deferential, who pulls him sideways into actual friendship by sheer extroverted persistence.

**Dynamic:** Storm, who lost a brother-figure when Reed went, recognizes that Lang has lost a daughter and that they are both running the same protective custodianship of the FF kids out of the same hole. The friendship is built laterally on parallel grief, never spoken aloud. They drink. Storm makes Lang laugh in a way few other adults do. Storm also flirts at Lang's expense, which Lang accepts as social glue.

**Trust / safety:** Genuine, with one specific carve-out: Lang does not let Storm meet Cassie until very late, and is visibly tense the first time the meeting occurs.

**Failure mode:** Storm's risk tolerance is too high; Lang sometimes follows him into operational stupidities he would not undertake alone, because Storm's confidence operates on Lang the same way mentor-approval does.

**Leverage:** Almost none from outside — they are too close. Internally, Storm has more access to Lang's actual feelings than any adult except possibly Cassie.

**Tells:** With Storm, Lang's self-deprecation becomes performative rather than defensive — a bit they do together — and his laughter is throat-out, not the closed-mouth half-snort he uses in most professional company.

---

### 5.B.7 — BENTLEY-23, ONOME, ALEX POWER, DRAGON MAN, AND THE MOLOID BROTHERS (Wards / Future Foundation Children)

These belong together. They are not individual relationships in the file; they are a *single sublimated paternal field.*

**Origin:** FF #1 (Fraction/Allred, 2012). Lang inherits custody.

**Dynamic:** Lang treats this collective of children — a Doom-clone, a brilliant young Black girl, a Power child, a half-tame android, three subterranean siblings — as exactly what they are to him: a chance to be the father he believes he failed to be, on harder difficulty, without the personal stakes that derange his judgment around Cassie. He is patient with them in ways he was never patient with himself. He cooks for them. He learns their dietary specifics. He memorizes Onome's school schedule. He referees Bentley-23's worst impulses with a calm that comes from knowing exactly what it costs a child to be told he is good when he is not sure he is.

**Special note on Bentley-23:** Functionally Lang's most psychologically loaded ward, because Bentley is a Doom-clone and Cassie's murderer is Doom. Lang's ability to love Bentley anyway is one of the genuinely heroic acts in his record, performed entirely without witness or acknowledgment.

**Trust / safety:** Total in the direction children → Lang. Lang in turn trusts the children with his own emotional state more than he trusts most adults — they get the unedited version because he assumes (correctly and condescendingly) that they will not weaponize it.

**Failure mode:** Over-identification. When a child is in danger Lang's tactical thinking narrows the same way it narrows for Cassie. The FF kids have been used as bait against him successfully.

**Leverage:** A credible threat to any of them produces immediate, total mobilization of Lang's resources and an absolute disregard for his own preservation.

**Tells:** Around the children, Lang sits down to be at eye level. He carries snacks in his pockets. He has, on record, repaired Bentley's eyewear three times rather than letting Bentley know it had broken in a way that required adult intervention.

---

### 5.B.8 — JAMES "RHODEY" RHODES (Lateral Peer, Achieved)

**Origin:** Stark-orbit professional acquaintance ripens into actual friendship across the 80s and intermittently after. Rhodes is one of very few characters Lang treats as a true equal almost from the start, because Rhodes — like Lang — knew Stark from underneath, as employee and as friend, and because Rhodes was never Lang's boss.

**Dynamic:** Quiet, durable, low-frequency. They do not call each other often. When they do, they pick up mid-conversation. Rhodes is one of the only people who can ask Lang a hard question about Cassie and get a straight answer.

**Trust / safety:** High. Of all his peer relationships, this one carries the least defensive scaffolding.

**Failure mode:** They share a tendency to under-report their own injuries to each other, which produces a feedback loop in which both men go undertreated longer than they should after combat exposure.

**Leverage:** None easily exploitable; the relationship is too private.

**Tells:** With Rhodes, Lang sometimes initiates contact without an instrumental reason — the only adult relationship in the file where this is reliably true.

---

### 5.B.9 — JANET VAN DYNE (Mentor-Adjacent / Conscience-Object)

**Origin:** Early Avengers period. Janet's relationship to Pym makes her presence in Lang's life unavoidable from the start; her independent stature as Wasp makes her one of the few people who can issue judgments on Lang's Ant-Man conduct that he cannot dismiss.

**Dynamic:** Warm, protective, slightly maternal, with an edge of vigilance — Janet has watched one man self-destruct inside this technology and is alert to early warning signs in the second. Lang understands he is being monitored and is grateful for it.

**Trust / safety:** Lang trusts Janet to call him out and silently relies on her to do so. He does not socially lean on her because he reads her as Pym's, and overstepping that line is unthinkable.

**Failure mode:** Lang will under-report Particle-related symptoms to Janet specifically, because he does not want to confirm her fear that history is repeating.

**Leverage:** Janet's opinion is one of the few female-coded authority signals Lang weights heavily; she can route his behavior with a single sentence.

**Tells:** He stands when she enters a room. He has done this since the 80s. He is not aware he is doing it.

---

### 5.B.10 — CLINT BARTON (Failed Peer Bond / Mirror Refused)

**Origin:** Avengers tenures, overlapping intermittently across decades.

**Dynamic:** This is a relationship that *should* work — two scrappy, working-class, morally compromised heroes with carceral-adjacent backgrounds and chronic financial problems — and conspicuously does not stabilize. The diagnostic value is high. Lang sees in Barton the version of himself he refuses to become: the man who has stopped pretending the criminality bothers him. Barton sees in Lang the version of himself that has not yet given up trying. Each is faintly disappointed by the other and neither will say so.

**Trust / safety:** Operational only. They will cover each other in combat without hesitation. They will not have dinner.

**Failure mode:** Under stress they each privately judge the other's choices, and the judgment leaks as sarcasm. Neither apologizes.

**Leverage:** Each could route the other's behavior through shame more effectively than almost anyone else, and neither will use it, because they each suspect they would deserve the same in return.

**Tells:** Lang's humor is sharper around Barton, slightly more cutting, less self-deprecating — he allows himself a meanness in Barton's presence he allows himself nowhere else.

---

### 5.B.11 — KATE BISHOP, AMERICA CHAVEZ, BILLY KAPLAN, TOMMY SHEPHERD (Young Avengers / Cassie's Peer Group)

**Origin:** *Avengers: The Children's Crusade.* Lang is resurrected into a world where Cassie has friends — adult-coded, hero-coded, his daughter's chosen family.

**Dynamic:** Lang's relationship with each of these young heroes is filtered through the fact that they knew Cassie better, more recently, than he did. After her death in the same arc, they become carriers of her — every conversation with them is partially a conversation with the part of his daughter's life he missed. He is unfailingly kind to them. He remembers their birthdays. He treats Wiccan with a specific extra tenderness because Wiccan is the one who brought him back, and Lang carries a debt there he cannot articulate without crying.

**Trust / safety:** High in both directions. They trust him as a senior adult who will not patronize them; he trusts them as the closest available access to who his daughter was becoming.

**Failure mode:** Lang will overextend protection toward this group in a way that occasionally embarrasses them, and he will not stop because the alternative is feeling the absence directly.

**Leverage:** A threat to any of them lands with Cassie-grade priority in his decision tree.

**Tells:** When any of them is in the room, Lang's eye drift confirms a quick visual head-count, repeated at intervals. He does not do this with adult teammates.

---

### 5.B.12 — DARREN CROSS (Anti-Relationship / Origin Antagonist) — see hostile-actor file. Noted here because Cross's *daughter* and the parallel of two fathers willing to do anything for a child is the structural mirror Lang has never let himself look at directly. The avoidance is diagnostic.

---

### 5.B.13 — ERIC O'GRADY (Successor / Shadow / Cautionary Mirror)

**Origin:** Third Ant-Man, irredeemable in ways Lang never quite was. Their relationship is brief and tense.

**Dynamic:** Lang treats O'Grady with a combination of professional disgust and uncomfortable recognition. O'Grady is what an Ant-Man without conscience looks like — a control case for Lang's own moral architecture. Lang's revulsion is partly self-protective; he cannot afford to find O'Grady sympathetic.

**Trust / safety:** None. Operationally hostile.

**Failure mode:** Lang's contempt for O'Grady is one of the few places he allows himself moral superiority, and the superiority is a defense against recognizing that O'Grady's path was available to him at several junctures.

**Leverage:** Anyone framing Lang as "the better Ant-Man" can secure his cooperation more easily than anyone framing him as the *good* one.

---

### 5.B.14 — NADIA VAN DYNE (Protégé / Lineage Continuation)

**Origin:** Late-Pym-era introduction; intensifies post-Pym.

**Dynamic:** Lang treats Nadia as Pym's daughter — which she is — and therefore as someone toward whom he owes the original debt extended forward one generation. He is patient, encouraging, scrupulously non-condescending about her intellect (which exceeds his own and which he readily acknowledges). He is, in effect, paying Pym back through her.

**Trust / safety:** High and clean. The relationship is not complicated by guilt because Nadia did not know Lang during his worst periods.

**Failure mode:** He will overstate her father's stability to her, because he believes she deserves the inheritance of a less-broken Pym than the one history actually delivered.

**Leverage:** Anyone threatening Nadia activates both the Pym-debt and the FF-kids protective field; the response is layered and total.

---

### 5.B.15 — THE ANTS (Non-Human / Diagnostic)

Included because the file would be incomplete without them and because the behavioral pattern is real.

**Dynamic:** Lang names individual ants. He grieves them when they die in operations. He has on multiple occasions diverted from mission objectives to extract a wounded ant. Crewmen-tier insects receive eulogies, sometimes vocalized aloud when he believes himself unobserved.

**Diagnostic value:** Confirms the protective-paternal field as a generalized rather than person-specific drive. The man cannot let a creature he has commanded die anonymously. Cassie's death is the obvious referent; the ant-grief is a low-cost outlet for an otherwise lethal-volume bereavement.

**Tells:** He talks to them. In private. In full sentences. Sometimes to apologize.

---

### 5.B.16 — STRUCTURAL OBSERVATIONS ACROSS THE NETWORK

A pattern visible only at the network level, not at the level of any individual bond:

- **Vertical bonds dominate.** Eleven of the relationships above are structurally non-lateral. Three (Storm, Rhodes, the failed-mirror with Barton) approach peer status; only two (Storm, Rhodes) stabilize there.
- **Female bonds are sparse and over-deferential.** Janet, Nadia, Kate, America, the female FF wards. The pattern is consistent: respect, protection, careful distance. The Cassie-cathexis appears to occupy the bandwidth that would otherwise organize adult-female intimacy.
- **He does not have a best friend in the colloquial sense.** He has a daughter, a creditor (Pym), a confessor he will not confess to (Rogers), a brother-in-grief he will not name as such (Storm), and a quiet equal he does not call often enough (Rhodes). The absence of a single high-frequency, low-stakes adult friendship is the most striking lacuna in the file.
- **Every relationship in this section is a transaction in grace.** He is either repaying someone for forgiving him, extending forgiveness forward to someone younger, or refusing to enter relationships where the question of forgiveness does not arise. The ledger is the architecture.

The leverage map that emerges: route through Pym for ethical course-correction, through Rogers for refusal-of-compromise, through Cassie or the FF children for total mobilization, through Storm for honest emotional access, through Rhodes for genuine truth-telling. Avoid Barton as a channel — he will be heard but not heeded. Avoid Stark-invocations now that


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*Compiled cross-reference to canonical record, 1979–present. Each subject mapped longitudinally: origin of relation, behavioral signature in interaction, evolution across decades, trust-and-safety topology (what Lang will let them do to him, what he will not), characteristic failure mode, and exploitable leverage. The file is granular by design; the texture of an intimate enemy is in the small things.*

---

## I. ENEMIES & RIVALS

### Darren Cross — *the original mark, the recurring shadow*

**Origin (1979, Marvel Premiere #47–48):** Cross is the cardiologically failing industrialist who kidnaps Dr. Sondheim and is, in the literal sense, the proximate cause of Lang's becoming Ant-Man. Lang infiltrates Cross Technological Enterprises in the original outing, frees Sondheim, and Cross is left for dead. He returns, repeatedly, gigantified by his own modified Pym Particle siphons; he is the inverse of Lang's relationship to the technology — Cross uses scale to dominate, Lang uses it to disappear.

**Behavioral signature in interaction:** Lang fights Cross with a controlled, almost surgical hostility he does not display toward any other adversary. There is no banter. The wisecracks that lubricate every other Ant-Man combat sequence go quiet. This is the only enemy Lang fights *without* the humor-armor; Cross is the man who once threatened Cassie's heart surgeon, and the affect goes flat and cold.

**Evolution:** Across forty-five years Cross resurrects in various corporate-villain configurations. Lang has never fully metabolized him as a defeated adversary — Cross's recurrent return functions, in the file, as the *anniversary reaction* of the founding trauma. Whenever Cross appears, Lang's tactical patience improves and his moral flexibility narrows. He becomes, briefly, the most disciplined version of himself.

**Trust/safety:** Zero. Lang assumes Cross will hurt the people Lang loves and plans accordingly. There is no negotiation surface.

**Failure mode:** Tunnel vision. When Cross is in play, Lang stops modeling third parties; he hunts. This has produced collateral damage and tactical mistakes (telegraphed entries, ignored backup).

**Leverage:** Cross's leverage on Lang is permanent and asymmetric — he is the only enemy who established the precedent that Lang's daughter can die because of an adult man's choices. Lang's leverage on Cross is technical: he understands Pym tech better than Cross ever will, and Cross knows it, which is why Cross's plans against Lang always involve overwhelming force rather than finesse.

---

### Crossfire (William Cross) — *the bounty, the back-of-the-neck threat*

**Origin:** Inherited from the Hank Pym rogues' gallery; reorients against Lang in the Spencer run when the bounty on Ant-Man's head becomes the connective tissue of the Miami era. Crossfire's whole technique is auditory-cortical manipulation — he literally gets into people's heads via sound.

**Dynamics:** Crossfire is the enemy who treats Lang as *a price*, not a person. This is psychologically corrosive in a specific way — Lang's self-schema already says he is the screw-up who got away with it, and Crossfire's pursuit confirms the suspicion that the universe has been waiting to collect. Lang's defensive humor *deepens* against Crossfire, not lightens; the jokes get blacker.

**Trust/safety:** Treats him as a procedural threat rather than a moral antagonist. Lang's countermeasures are technical (audio-shielded helmet variants, ant-relay perimeter alarms while sleeping) rather than personal.

**Failure mode:** Lang underestimates Crossfire's patience. Crossfire is a long-game enemy; Lang is a short-game thinker. He assumes the immediate confrontation ends the threat. It does not.

**Leverage:** Crossfire's leverage is *attention itself* — by existing as a standing bounty, he forces Lang into the kind of low-grade hypervigilance that depletes him over months. Lang's leverage on Crossfire is the same as it is on any psy-op adversary: Crossfire needs Lang to listen, and Lang has decades of practice not-listening to authority figures who tell him what to do.

---

### Taskmaster — *the photocopy, the mirror that makes him laugh*

**Origin/dynamics:** Recurrent in the Spencer Miami arc. Taskmaster's photographic-reflex mimicry is the perfect counter to a hero whose primary tactical asset is *unpredictability through small scale*. Lang loses straight fights with Taskmaster and knows it.

**Behavioral signature:** Notably, Lang likes Taskmaster on some submerged level. There's a recognition between two professionals who do dishonest work for understandable reasons. Lang's interactions with Taskmaster have the most morally-grey-banter density in the file — they negotiate, they trade, they sometimes work parallel jobs.

**Trust/safety:** Conditional. Lang will share a bar with him. Lang will not turn his back on him.

**Failure mode:** Lang's identification with the working-criminal frame makes him slow to escalate against Taskmaster. He treats Taskmaster as a colleague who will sometimes try to kill him, which is too generous a frame.

**Leverage:** Cash. Taskmaster is mercenary; Lang's leverage is the ability to pay or outbid. Taskmaster's leverage on Lang is that he has watched Lang fight enough times to predict him, and Lang knows this and finds it humiliating.

---

### Augustine Cross — *the inherited debt*

**Origin:** Darren's son, picks up the family business of trying to kill Ant-Man. The relationship is dynastic and tragic in the genre way.

**Dynamics:** Lang treats Augustine with a fatigued tenderness underneath the combat — the tenderness of a man who recognizes another man trapped in a father's template. Lang understands inheritance. He has spent his entire life trying not to bequeath his patterns to Cassie.

**Failure mode:** Lang hesitates against Augustine in a way he never does against Darren. The hesitation has nearly cost him.

**Leverage:** Augustine wants what Darren wanted (Pym tech, vengeance). Lang's leverage is appeal to the inheritance frame itself — Lang can sometimes talk Augustine down, briefly, by naming the trap.

---

### Power Broker (Curtiss Jackson, era-specific) — *the man who buys the rationalization*

**Origin:** Spencer-era Miami. Power Broker hires Lang for grey work, then blackmails him with leaked footage of the grey work. This is the most psychologically *exact* enemy in Lang's file because Power Broker did not coerce Lang into the original transgression — Lang chose it. Power Broker only weaponized the choice.

**Dynamics:** The relationship is a closed loop of Lang's own self-betrayal. Every interaction with Power Broker is a fresh demonstration that Lang's rationalization engine ("I have to do this for Cassie") is monetizable. Power Broker sees the engine and rents it.

**Trust/safety:** Lang trusts Power Broker exactly as far as the contract is paying. This is corrosive — Lang has *normalized* working with a man he despises, because the alternative is admitting he should not have started.

**Failure mode:** Lang cannot extricate himself cleanly. Every attempted exit produces a new round of blackmail. The relationship demonstrates Lang's catastrophic underestimation of second-order consequences; he thought he was taking one job.

**Leverage:** Power Broker's leverage is total — recorded evidence, financial dependency, social humiliation in front of Cassie. Lang's only leverage is the destruction-of-records option, which he eventually exercises, painfully, with cost.

---

### Doctor Doom — *the killer of the daughter*

**Origin:** *Avengers: The Children's Crusade* (2010–12). Doom kills Cassie.

**Dynamics:** There are not many of them, which is the point. Lang and Doom do not have a *relationship*; they have a single irreducible fact between them. Lang has, on the record, not avenged Cassie in any sustained personal vendetta arc — and this absence is itself diagnostic. Lang does not have the personality architecture for vengeance. His grief turns inward (the FF run, the protective-of-other-children sublimation) rather than outward. When Doom appears in subsequent stories, Lang's affect goes *quiet* — not cold like with Cross, but flat in a different register, a kind of grief-shutdown.

**Trust/safety:** No surface. Doom is not a person to Lang; Doom is the geometry of the worst day.

**Failure mode:** Lang dissociates around Doom. He does not strategize well in Doom-proximate operations because the cognitive load of being-in-the-same-room is consuming most of his processing.

**Leverage:** None either way that matters. Doom does not care about Lang. Lang cannot harm Doom and has, on some level, accepted this.

---

### Hank Pym — *the asterisked rival*

(Cross-referenced with *Relationships II — Mentors.* Listed here because the rivalry valence is real and underdiscussed.)

Pym is the founding *grace-giver* and also the man whose name Lang inherited without permission-of-equals. The rivalry is one-sided, sublimated, and lives mostly in Lang's interior — the persistent sense that he is the *understudy* Ant-Man, that any compliment he receives in the role is partially Pym's. Pym, for his part, has at times treated Lang with mild condescension and at times with genuine collegiality; the inconsistency tracks Pym's own well-documented instability.

**Failure mode:** Lang will defer to Pym on technical decisions where Lang's instincts are correct. The mentor-introjection overrides Lang's own judgment on Pym-tech matters, which is precisely where Lang's judgment is arguably superior because he uses the tech less recklessly.

**Leverage:** Pym's leverage on Lang is the entire premise of Lang's adult identity. Lang's leverage on Pym is, quietly, that Lang has done more public good with the suit than Pym did, and both of them know it, and neither will say it.

---

### Mitch Carson — *the institutional grudge*

**Origin/dynamics:** Recurrent S.H.I.E.L.D.-adjacent antagonist in modern continuity. Carson represents the bureaucratic-law-enforcement enmity that Lang's carceral history makes structurally inevitable — there is always someone in a tie who wants the ex-con back in custody on principle.

**Behavioral signature:** Lang treats institutional enemies with a *learned slipperiness*. Carson activates Lang's prison-learned authority-management protocols: deference at the surface, technical evasion underneath, never a direct confrontation if a sidestep is available.

**Failure mode:** Lang occasionally cooperates *too* much under institutional pressure — the old carceral instinct to talk one's way through, which can produce admissions he later regrets.

**Leverage:** Carson has procedural leverage. Lang has the leverage of being more competent than the bureaucracy assumes, and consistently using that asymmetry to leave the building before paperwork closes.

---

### Whirlwind, the small-time recurrents (Porcupine, Black Fox, Magician, Grizzly, et al.) — *the rogues' gallery as social circle*

Lang's persistent low-rent rogues' gallery functions, in the file, less as enemies than as a kind of *occupational community*. He defeats them, processes them, sees them again two years later, exchanges a nod, fights them again. He knows their kids' names. He has loaned at least one of them money post-incarceration (Spencer run, implied transactions). This is the *Bronx-bar* texture of the Ant-Man rogues file — a working-class villainy that Lang treats as colleagues with bad jobs.

**Failure mode:** He will not finish them when finishing them is warranted. The collegiality is a vulnerability.

**Leverage:** Mutual. They know each other's tells.

---

## II. INTIMATES & LOVERS

### Margaret "Peggy Rae" Burdick — *the foundational marriage, the original abandonment*

**Origin (canonical backstory, established Marvel Premiere #47):** Peggy Rae is the mother of Cassie, married to Lang during his Stark-engineering period. The marriage does not survive Lang's incarceration. She divorces him during his prison sentence and subsequently remarries (Blake Burdick), gaining custody of Cassie. Across the 1980s she is rendered as antagonistic in the way ex-wives of superheroes are stock-rendered — withholding visitation, skeptical of Lang's stability, allied with her new husband against Lang's parenting claims.

**Dynamics:** The relationship in the active record is post-marital and adversarial. The custody fights of the early Lang corpus install a permanent template: *Lang must prove fitness for fatherhood against a woman who has already concluded he cannot be trusted.* He never wins this argument cleanly. The pattern of *managing information to avoid disappointing Cassie* — central to the eventual Cassie-rupture — was rehearsed first in managing information to avoid losing custody from Peggy Rae.

**Trust/safety:** Lang trusts Peggy Rae's love for Cassie absolutely. He does not trust her judgment of him, and has stopped trying to revise it.

**Evolution:** After her death (which the canon eventually delivers — Cassie loses her mother as well as, temporarily, her father), the antagonism becomes retrospectively unresolvable. Lang's behavioral pattern post-Peggy-Rae's-death is to *speak well of her to Cassie* in a way that exceeds his actual feelings — a generous post-mortem editing job that is itself an act of parenting.

**Failure mode:** With Peggy Rae alive, Lang's failure mode was to underpromise so as not to disappoint and then *also* underdeliver, producing exactly the disappointment he was managing for. With Peggy Rae deceased, his failure mode is the survivor's-revision — he has constructed a version of her that is more sympathetic than the lived record, and he occasionally references this constructed version to Cassie in ways Cassie can tell are constructed.

**Leverage:** She had total leverage during the custody years (court-recognized, socially-recognized, morally-recognized). She has retroactive leverage now — she is the woman who saw him at his worst, and Lang's interior monologue still occasionally addresses her.

---

### The Wasp (Janet Van Dyne) — *the unconsummated, the gentle non-event*

**Origin/dynamics:** A flirtation that lives, across the 80s Avengers years, in glances, mild banter, and the asymmetry of her station relative to his. Jan is glamorous, monied, central; Scott is the ex-con guest member. The non-development is the data.

**Behavioral signature:** Lang's interaction with Jan shows the precise pattern this file has flagged elsewhere — *vertical bonds form, lateral romantic bonds do not.* He cannot stop positioning her above himself, and that positioning is sexually inert in his interior. He treats her with a courteous deference that forecloses the very intimacy the surface flirtation seemed to invite.

**Trust/safety:** High platonic trust, especially regarding Cassie — Jan is one of the women in Lang's orbit he would entrust Cassie to without hesitation.

**Failure mode:** He could not initiate. He waited for her to make it real, and she had her own life.

**Leverage:** Neither sought leverage. The relationship is one of the cleanest in the file because nothing was ever risked.

---

### Jessica Jones — *the brief, the wrong, the honest*

**Origin (Bendis-era cross-reference):** A brief involvement during the early Alias/post-Alias period. Two people with overlapping carceral-and-recovery psychologies finding each other for a short window.

**Dynamics:** What is interesting in the file: with Jessica, Lang showed *less* of the deference pattern. Jessica did not present as a vertical figure to be deferred to — she presented as a damaged peer, and Lang could meet her there. The brief intimacy showed what Lang's romantic register looks like when the mentor-deference dynamic is absent. It was warmer, blunter, more adult.

**Trust/safety:** Mutual, conditional, professional in its honesty.

**Failure mode:** Neither pursued continuation. The cathexis on Cassie crowded Lang out; Jessica had her own trajectory. The non-continuation was sane.

**Leverage:** None sought. Both parties were too tired for games.

---

### Beth Sondheim (and the Sondheim-orbit relationships) — *the gratitude-tinged*

Across the canon, Lang's intimate orbit includes several relationships organized around women he met *through Cassie's medical world* or through subsequent extensions of that network. The pattern is consistent and worth naming: Lang's eros is most easily activated by women who are already coded *good-for-Cassie* in his frame. This is not pathology — it is the consequence of a personality wholly organized around a single primary attachment. He cannot fall for a woman his daughter would not approve of, because the disapproval would activate his core schema.

**Failure mode:** The Cassie-frame on the romantic partner is suffocating to the partner over time — they realize they are auditioning for a role they did not apply for.

**Leverage:** None sought. These relationships dissolve from neglect rather than conflict.

---

### Veronica King and the Miami social register — *the small-business-era flirtations*

**Origin (Spencer run):** Post-relocation to Miami, Lang's social life expands into a Florida register — adjacent women in the Ant-Man Security Solutions client base, neighbors, the occasional client-relationship-blurring. The Miami era is the most romantically active stretch of Lang's adult record.

**Dynamics:** Lang in Miami is *trying* — actively dating, actively presenting an adult life. The dating is unsuccessful in the same way the small business is unsuccessful and for the same root reason: he cannot sustain the honesty required because his secondary economy (Power Broker work) cannot be disclosed.

**Trust/safety:** Provisional. Lang gives his partners 70% of his life and tells himself that is enough.

**Failure mode:** The 30% he withholds is exactly the 30% that makes a relationship real, and his partners reliably notice that he is holding something back.

**Leverage:** Several of these women became, intentionally or not, vectors for the Crossfire bounty network (people Lang dated were leaned on for information). Lang's hypervigilance about *who his partners might be talking to* increased across the Miami years and contributed to terminating relationships that were not actually compromised.

---

### Cassie's mother-figures (parsed) — *the asymptote of a substitute*

Across the run, the file shows Lang gravitating, with imperfect awareness, toward women who could plausibly *co-parent* Cassie. He has never named this pattern, and several of the women have. He is not cynical about it — he is genuinely seeking partnership — but the partnership-criterion is so dominated by the Cassie-suitability variable that romantic chemistry runs a distant second. The result, across decades, is a series of relationships that look on paper like they should work and feel, in lived time, like job interviews for a position the candidate did not know was open.

---

### The non-relationship with romance generally — *the structural note*

This file must record what the canon shows: Lang has had no sustained romantic partnership of comparable depth to his paternal partnership. The longitudinal record across forty-five publication years contains numerous flirtations, two brief involvements of substance, and one failed marriage that pre-dates his costumed life. The *absence* of a deep adult partnership is the load-bearing fact in this section. It is consistent with the psychological formulation: a personality organized around one primary attachment cannot easily make room for a second of equal weight. He has, in effect, traded the possibility of romantic depth for the certainty of paternal devotion, and the trade was unconscious, and it was, in his own private accounting, worth it.

**Failure mode (aggregate):** Will not let a woman become as important as his daughter, and the women can feel it.

**Leverage (aggregate):** Anyone who genuinely wanted to harm Lang through an intimate partner has had thin material to work with — there is no current partner of sufficient depth to constitute a hostage. This is, paradoxically, one of his stronger operational securities, and it is also the loneliest fact in the file.

---

*— END SECTION. Cross-reference: Relationships I (Family — Cassie, Peggy Rae deceased, extended), Relationships II (Mentors & Peers — Pym, Stark, Richards, Rogers, FF cohort), Relationships IV (Mentees & the Young Avengers vector).*


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU DOSSIER — SCOTT EDWARD HARRIS LANG
## Section 6: Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

---

### 6.0 Framing

Scott Lang is, almost uniquely in the Marvel operational record, a subject whose *powers are entirely external* — the suit, the particles, the helmet, the ants — and whose *abilities are entirely internal* — engineering, burglary, tactical improvisation, dad-grade interpersonal disarmament. The split is itself diagnostic. He does not experience his power-set as part of him. He experiences it as borrowed, on loan from a better man (Pym), the way he experiences his redemption itself. Every operational pattern that follows in this section recurs to that fact.

This section is organized: (A) the loaned apparatus, (B) the native skill stack, (C) the integration profile — how the two combine in field use, including signature applications, recurring failure modes, and what the operational tells reveal about the psychology.

---

### 6.1 THE LOANED APPARATUS — Pym Particles, the Suit, the Helmet

#### 6.1.1 Pym Particles: Mechanism

Pym Particles are a subatomic field-effect that displaces or accretes mass through extradimensional shunting (Kosmos-dimension reservoir, per Pym's own published mechanics). Lang carries the particles in canister form on the belt of the suit — historically two color-coded canisters (red for shrink, blue for grow, though writers vary), reloaded between deployments. The particles are inhaled, absorbed transdermally through the suit's interior lining, or, in some later configurations, released directly into the size-shift field generated by the helmet/belt system.

**What Lang understands about them:** Most of it. He is one of perhaps six people alive who can actually maintain the suit at the component level — he has rebuilt the regulator, fabricated replacement canisters under field conditions, and on at least two documented occasions modified the particle-flow rate to compensate for damaged hardware. He is not Pym (cannot synthesize the particles from scratch — that knowledge Pym has never shared and Lang has never asked for, a deference pattern worth noting). He is the apparatus's *competent journeyman*, not its inventor.

**What he does not fully understand:** Long-term cumulative-exposure effects on his own nervous system. He has either declined to investigate or has investigated quietly and not recorded findings. Given the Pym-Yellowjacket precedent, this avoidance is notable.

#### 6.1.2 Size-Shift: Range and Limits

- **Lower bound (operational):** Roughly ½ inch — standard ant-scale, his default. He can hold this for hours.
- **Lower bound (extended):** Microverse-scale and below. He has gone subatomic in confirmed canon (mirroring Pym's and Janet's traversals). At true subatomic scale, conventional physics decoheres around him; survival is a matter of suit integrity and luck.
